The Wednesday meetings are where Eleanor does the work of being who she actually is. They are short — rarely more than forty minutes, frequently under twenty. They are conducted in the study with the door closed and, on certain occasions, with a small white-noise device running on the desk that is not present on other days. The persons attending are not introduced to staff and do not return frequently enough to become known. Some are legal. Some are not. Some are emissaries from interests that, were they named in the public calendar, would constitute their own news event.

Wednesdays are when the ledger is updated in its real currency. The rest of the week is the visible surface on top of the Wednesday substrate.

---

### 11. Seasonal and Anniversary Patterns

Beyond the weekly cycle, observation across a year reveals seasonal regularities.

She leaves the city for parts of August — a standing arrangement with a particular house in a particular town, used by the family for many years. The August trip is the period of the year she comes closest to inactivity, though "inactivity" for Eleanor still includes a paper diary and a daily call schedule.

She does not travel for the winter holidays in the way many of her peers do; she remains in the city and performs the holiday observances in compressed, formal form. The choice not to migrate south in January is, in context, a choice to remain available — to Kate, in principle, and to the counterparties who matter.

Certain dates each year produce measurable changes in her affect and behavior. The anniversary of her *first* return to visible life — not a public date, but one she tracks — falls in a specific window and is reliably preceded by several days of reduced sleep and increased Wednesday-style activity. Kate's birthday produces the annual touch noted above and a flatter, quieter day overall. There is one further date, in late autumn, on which she goes to a particular place alone, by car service under the non-primary name, and returns within four hours; what occurs at the place is not in the record. The regularity of the trip is, however, total.

She does not mark Derek-related dates in any observable way. Whatever Derek is to her now, he is not commemorated.

---

### 12. What a Quarter of Surveillance Would Conclude

A team coming off ninety days on Eleanor Bishop would file the following operational summary:

Subject is a creature of intense routine masquerading as a creature of social fluidity. The fluid surface is genuine in its execution and false in its meaning; the routines under it are the real life. Subject's operational security is analog, deliberate, and well-matched to her actual threat environment. Subject's affective range is narrow in observable behavior and almost certainly wider in unobserved interior. Subject's relationship to her estranged daughter has been ritualized rather than relinquished and constitutes an active, continuous, low-amplitude project conducted across multiple modalities. Subject is approachable through a small number of trusted intermediaries; subject is not approachable directly without prior arrangement that subject controls.

The most exploitable feature of the daily pattern is its regularity itself. Eleanor's discipline is her armor and her exposure. A person who knows what time she walks home from the Thursday lunch, which corner she crosses, which way she enters her building, has a degree of access to her that her wealth and her caution do not, ultimately, prevent. She knows this. She has decided that the cost of changing the pattern would exceed the cost of being predictable, because the predictability is also what allows her to *do the work*, which is, in the final accounting, what she has organized her entire day around — the work of running the ledger, of keeping the arrangement, of remaining, in her own internal taxonomy, the woman who held it all together while others acted out.

She gets up at 5:50. She drinks her water. She makes her own coffee. She sits at the island and reads the papers standing. She is dressed by 8:00. The rest follows.


## Relationships I — Family & Origin Bonds

# RELATIONSHIPS I — FAMILY & ORIGIN BONDS

A surveillance-grade map of the people whose existence shaped, constrained, and was instrumentalized by Eleanor Bishop. The principle to keep in mind throughout: Eleanor does not have *relationships* in the ordinary affective sense; she has **positions** — durable arrangements with specific people whose terms she renegotiates as conditions change. Even her loves are positions. This does not make the loves false. It makes them administered.

---

## 1. DEREK BISHOP — Husband (status: deceased per published canon; *operationally*: the founding counterparty)

### History

Eleanor's marriage to Derek Bishop is the originating financial and psychological transaction of her adult life and the template from which every later arrangement (Madame Masque, the Eleanor-of-the-shadows period, the staged reentry into Kate's life) is drawn. The published record does not give us the courtship in scene, but its residue is everywhere: a young woman who married into Bishop Publishing money and into Derek's particular flavor of cold-corruption wealth, who bore two daughters inside that marriage, and who eventually faked her own death rather than divorce him. *That last fact is the diagnostic one.* Divorce was available to a woman of her means; she chose obliteration of self instead. The reason has to be that divorce did not solve the actual problem. Derek did not merely make her unhappy. Derek *owned the field she stood on* — financially, legally, socially, and (in the Masque-adjacent reveal) criminally — to a degree that ordinary legal exit could not unwind.

### Dynamics

The marriage operated, in its functional years, as a two-principal partnership in image management. Eleanor was the *front of house* — the charity boards, the gala faces, the parental presence at school functions when one was required, the warmth that made the Bishop name reputable in rooms where Derek's coldness would have repelled. Derek was the *back of house* — the money, the criminality, the deals neither of them spoke about over dinner. This division of labor is the basis for every adult skill Eleanor later weaponized: she learned in this marriage how to be the visible, sympathetic, competent woman who keeps a household running while pretending not to know what funds it. She also learned, by long observation of Derek, how to think in leverage rather than in feeling.

### Evolution

The relationship moved through three distinguishable phases. **Phase one (the working marriage):** functional alliance, real if conditional affection, two children, the construction of a shared front. **Phase two (the corrosion):** at some point — the record is suggestive rather than explicit — Derek's behavior crossed a line Eleanor could not absorb. Possibilities consistent with canon include direct violence toward her, a threat to the daughters, or the discovery that Derek had compromised her in ways she could not undo through normal channels. The specifics matter less than the result: Eleanor concluded the marriage could not be exited intact. **Phase three (the ghost years):** Eleanor disappears via staged death, Derek continues as widower-patriarch, Eleanor surveils from outside, the marriage becomes a one-way observation post. With Derek's eventual death the position closes; Eleanor returns to the field he had occupied.

### Trust / Safety

Zero, in either direction, by the end. The startling thing is how high baseline trust must have been at the start for the partnership to have functioned as well as it did for as long as it did. Eleanor is not a woman who marries men she does not believe she can manage. She married Derek believing she could; the faked death is the admission that she was wrong. *That admission is the only one of its kind in her record.* She has not been wrong about a man since.

### Failure Mode

The marriage failed in the specific way that all of Eleanor's primary attachments fail: the counterparty's behavior exceeded the variance her management model could absorb, and rather than escalate inside the arrangement she exited the arrangement entirely while preserving as much of its asset base as possible. The faked death is *not* a panic response. It is a controlled write-off.

### Leverage

Posthumously, Derek remains leverage on Eleanor in two senses. (1) **Narrative leverage:** Eleanor's entire returning-mother story to Kate is structured around Derek-as-monster, Eleanor-as-fugitive-protector. Any evidence that complicates this binary — that Eleanor was a co-architect rather than a victim — collapses her standing with Kate. The Masque arc supplies exactly such evidence. (2) **Forensic leverage:** the financial and criminal entanglements Derek built are unlikely to have been fully unwound; Eleanor inherited the residue, including obligations she did not personally contract. An adversary who reconstructs the Bishop ledger from Derek's era can find Eleanor inside it.

---

## 2. KATE BISHOP — Daughter (status: estranged-with-contact; *operationally*: the central unresolved position)

### History

Kate is Eleanor's younger daughter and, by every available marker, her favorite — though "favorite" in Eleanor's vocabulary means *the one in whom I am most invested*, which is not the same thing as *the one I am best for*. Eleanor was present for Kate's early childhood. She was the warm parent, the one Kate retained an idealized image of after the supposed death. Then, at an age sufficiently young that Kate's developing self organized around the absence, Eleanor staged her death and disappeared. Kate's adolescence — the home invasion and sexual assault at the Met gala, the years of grief, the turn to archery as a discipline of agency, the construction of Hawkeye as an identity built explicitly *not* on Bishop money — all of it happened in a world Eleanor had chosen to vacate.

The faked-death decision is the foundational fact of this relationship, and the one Eleanor cannot honestly account for. *No version of the duress narrative explains the absence of a contingency that would have protected Kate.* Eleanor could have surveilled Kate from outside (she did); she could have engineered protective interventions (she did not, conspicuously, during the Met assault); she could have made provision for Kate to know, when adult, that her mother was alive. She made none of these provisions because the operation was optimized for Eleanor's survival, not for Kate's developmental wellbeing. Eleanor's later framing of the absence as protective is the cleanest single example of her rationalization architecture in the record.

### Dynamics

Returning-Eleanor's behavior toward Kate is a remarkably consistent four-beat loop, repeated across multiple arcs:

1. **Approach with warmth and resource.** A gift, an offer, a meal, an introduction, a problem solved before Kate asked. The approach is always calibrated to a specific Kate-need that Eleanor has correctly identified (rent, a case, a contact, validation).
2. **Insertion of condition.** Once Kate has accepted, a condition appears — a piece of information Eleanor needs, a person Kate should stop seeing, a case Kate should drop, a role Kate should accept.
3. **Performance of wounded love when condition is refused.** Eleanor does not threaten. She *grieves* at Kate, audibly and visibly, recasting Kate's refusal as cruelty.
4. **Tightening through indirection.** When the wound-display fails, Eleanor escalates through intermediaries — lawyers, family (Susan), mutual acquaintances, financial pressure on Kate's adjacent relationships rather than on Kate directly.

The loop's brilliance, and its pathology, is that each cycle resets Kate's emotional ledger toward debt: Eleanor has *given*, Kate has *refused*, therefore Kate is the one who owes repair. Kate's slow education over Thompson's runs is the education of learning to refuse the framing as well as the gift.

### Evolution

The arc from reunion to estrangement is not a single break but a series of *recognitions*, each of which Kate stages with increasing clarity. Early Kate accepts Eleanor's terms gratefully; mid-arc Kate accepts terms while protesting them; the Masque-revelation Kate refuses the terms and names the structure; estranged Kate maintains the title of daughter while refusing the position of managed asset. Eleanor's trajectory across the same period is a study in failure to update: she keeps offering the same loop with cosmetic variations, because she has no other vocabulary for being a mother. She has not learned, by latest publication, to want a relationship with Kate that does not include managing her.

### Trust / Safety

From Kate's side: *titled but not functional.* Kate will answer a call. Kate will not believe what is said on it without verification. Kate has installed permanent internal defenses against Eleanor's loop — she now notices the gift-with-condition structure as it begins and can name it in real time. This is the developmental work of an adult child of a covertly controlling parent and it is, by the latest published material, well advanced in Kate.

From Eleanor's side: Eleanor *trusts* Kate in the specific sense that she believes Kate will not move to destroy her, and this is correct. Eleanor does not trust Kate in the sense of believing Kate's stated boundaries are real — she persistently behaves as though the next gift, the next crisis, the next family event will reopen the door, and is genuinely surprised when it does not.

### Failure Mode

The relationship fails the same way each cycle: Eleanor confuses access for love and treats Kate's withdrawal of access as a wound requiring repair through *more access*, when the actual repair would require Eleanor to tolerate distance without managing it. She cannot. The failure mode is therefore *recurrence* — the relationship does not break, it loops, and each loop slightly worsens Kate's baseline willingness to engage.

### Leverage

Kate is Eleanor's single largest exposure. (1) **Affective:** Eleanor's self-concept requires the maternal frame; Kate is the only person who can legitimately revoke it, and partial revocation is already underway. (2) **Reputational:** Kate's friends and chosen family (Clint Barton, the Young Avengers cohort, the West Coast cohort) constitute a network that knows Eleanor's pattern and will not be deceived by it; Eleanor's social field in Kate's world is already cauterized. (3) **Operational:** Kate is a competent investigator with personal motive to reconstruct Eleanor's history. Any adversary who wishes to compromise Eleanor through Kate need not turn Kate against her — Kate is already turned; they need only supply Kate with documentation Kate has not yet sought.

---

## 3. SUSAN BISHOP — Sister-in-law (Derek's sister) (status: ongoing tactical alliance / family-channel intermediary)

### History

Susan is Derek's sister, Kate's aunt, and the surviving Bishop of the elder generation in the published record. Her relationship to Eleanor predates the marriage's collapse: two women installed into the same family by different mechanisms (Eleanor by marriage, Susan by birth), navigating an environment dominated by Derek's coldness and the family's money. The historical record shows the two operating as something between allies and rivals — collaborative on questions of Bishop image and family management, competitive on questions of standing and influence with Kate.

### Dynamics

In the post-return period Susan functions as Eleanor's most reliable *intermediary channel* to Kate. When Kate refuses Eleanor's direct contact, Eleanor reaches Kate through Susan — a phone call, a family event, a piece of concerning news passed sideways. Susan is not Eleanor's puppet; she has her own relationship with Kate and her own interests in the family's continuity. But Susan's continuity-interest aligns often enough with Eleanor's access-interest that the channel functions. Eleanor cultivates this channel with the same care she gives any operational asset: regular contact, shared confidences (calibrated, never complete), small material kindnesses, the performance of sisterly intimacy that does not require Susan to fully understand what Eleanor is doing.

### Evolution

The Susan-Eleanor relationship's evolution tracks the family's financial and legal evolution: as Derek's estate, the publishing empire's residue, and the various Bishop trusts moved through probate and litigation, Eleanor and Susan converged tactically because their economic interests overlapped against external claimants. The faked-death period strained the relationship in ways the record does not fully detail — Susan would have grieved a sister-in-law who was not dead — but the post-return reconciliation appears to have been pragmatic on both sides. Susan accepted the duress framing because the alternative was unworkable family rupture.

### Trust / Safety

*Conditional and bounded on both sides.* Susan trusts Eleanor to act consistently in defense of Bishop interests; Susan does not trust Eleanor with information that could be turned against her. Eleanor trusts Susan to maintain the family-channel function; Eleanor does not trust Susan with the Masque-adjacent material or with the true accounting of the faked-death period. The two women have, in effect, signed a non-disclosure agreement neither has spoken aloud.

### Failure Mode

The alliance fails if Susan's interests diverge sharply from Eleanor's — for instance, if Kate aligns with Susan against Eleanor on a question of family standing or inheritance, Susan will choose Kate, because Kate is the future of the name and Eleanor is the residue of the past. Eleanor knows this and manages Susan accordingly, never asking for more than Susan can give without forcing the choice.

### Leverage

Susan is leverage on Eleanor only insofar as Susan knows things. Her actual evidentiary holdings about Eleanor's bad acts are likely smaller than Eleanor fears and larger than Susan realizes. A sophisticated investigator who deposed Susan with the right documents would extract material Susan herself does not know she is holding — the small inconsistencies of dates, the names of intermediaries from the faked-death period, the financial transactions Eleanor routed through Bishop-family vehicles. Eleanor's management of Susan is, in part, the prevention of this deposition by keeping Susan's affection sufficient to inhibit her cooperation with hostile parties.

---

## 4. THE ELDER DAUGHTER — Kate's sister (status: peripheral in published record; *operationally*: the comparative case)

### History

Kate has a sister in the published Bishop family record — older, less central to the Hawkeye narrative, more aligned with Derek's social world and the Bishop family's conventional life. The publication record gives her thin treatment, which is itself diagnostic of Eleanor's parental allocation: Eleanor's *operational* investment is in Kate, not in the elder daughter. This is not the same as preference in love. It is preference in *project*.

### Dynamics

The elder daughter appears to have absorbed the Bishop family's expectations along conventional lines — money, marriage, social position — and to have required less of Eleanor's management precisely because she was already inside the structure Eleanor would have wanted her in. Kate, by contrast, broke from the structure (refusing the money, building Hawkeye, choosing a chosen-family of mostly unsuitable allies), and so Kate became the project. Eleanor's behavior toward the elder daughter is therefore *less visible in the record* not because it is healthier but because it is less effortful: a daughter inside the frame requires less framing.

### Evolution

The faked-death period affected both daughters but differently; the elder daughter's response is less documented and likely less disorganizing because her identity was less constructed around Eleanor's presence-or-absence. Post-return, Eleanor's relationship with the elder daughter is closer to conventional adult-mother-and-daughter civility than her relationship with Kate, which is one of the reasons Kate experiences Eleanor's reentry as singular: Eleanor reaches for Kate with the intensity of unfinished business.

### Trust / Safety

Probably higher in baseline than Eleanor-Kate, precisely because lower in stakes. The elder daughter has less to lose from Eleanor and less to gain; the relationship sits at a lower temperature where neither party is testing the other.

### Failure Mode

The elder daughter is a potential failure mode for *Eleanor's narrative* rather than for the relationship itself: if Kate ever sought the elder daughter's account of the family-of-origin years, the elder daughter might confirm details Eleanor has reframed. The two daughters' memories of the marriage and the mother almost certainly differ, and the difference is information.

### Leverage

Low direct, moderate indirect. The elder daughter is leverage on Eleanor primarily as a corroborating witness who has not yet been asked the right questions.

---

## 5. ELEANOR'S FAMILY OF ORIGIN — Parents, the unrecorded antecedents (status: off-page; *operationally*: the structural ghost)

### History

The published record does not name Eleanor's parents, give us her maiden name with any forensic weight, or describe the household she came from. This silence is, for an analytical workup, the most important *absence* in the file. Eleanor entered the Bishop family already formed — already capable of marrying Derek with eyes open, already fluent in the wealthy-front-of-house performance, already structured to convert intimacy into leverage. None of this is innate. It was learned somewhere.

### Inferred Dynamics

The behavior pattern Eleanor exhibits as an adult — attachment to powerful, dangerous men; negotiation of female agency from inside rather than outside the orbit of male power; identification of love with usefulness; rationalization of one's own conduct as forced by others — is the signature of a family-of-origin in which (a) love was conditional on performance, (b) the mother (or maternal figure) modeled this same pattern in the prior generation, and (c) the daughter's escape route was understood to be a *good marriage* rather than an *independent life*. Eleanor's marriage to Derek reads, in this light, not as a mistake but as a *successful execution of the script she was raised to execute*. The mistake came later, when the script proved insufficient to manage him.

The Masque alliance is the second-order version of the same pattern: when the marriage-as-protection failed, Eleanor reached for another powerful figure to negotiate with rather than for a horizontal solidarity with peers or for institutional protection (police, courts, social services). This is learned helplessness *inside* a sophisticated competence — she knows how to negotiate with predators because that is the negotiation her family of origin trained her for.

### Trust / Safety

Indeterminate. Whoever they were, they are likely deceased or estranged by the time of the published record; Eleanor does not reach for them under stress, does not invoke them in self-narrative, does not appear to use family-of-origin resources in any operation. They are functionally absent from her operational life and present only as the *structure* of her assumptions.

### Failure Mode

The family-of-origin's failure mode is not active — they cannot, at this point, fail Eleanor further than they already have. The failure is sedimented in her template and cannot be reversed without psychotherapeutic work she shows no inclination to undertake.

### Leverage

For an investigator: enormous. A reconstruction of Eleanor's maiden identity and family-of-origin record would supply the missing etiological documentation — early-life addresses, school records, parental occupations, sibling relationships, the precise socioeconomic register she came from, and most consequentially the *names of any prior alliances or arrangements* that might have followed her into adulthood. Eleanor's faked-death period required infrastructure; some of that infrastructure may have drawn on family-of-origin connections she has otherwise kept walled off. This is the single most under-explored vein in the published record and the one a hostile intelligence apparatus would mine first.

---

## 6. THE DEAD — Derek, and the version of Eleanor who died

### The dead husband

Derek functions, posthumously, as both *exculpation* and *liability* in Eleanor's narrative architecture. As exculpation: every bad act of Eleanor's can be routed back to Derek's prior bad acts, and Derek is conveniently unavailable to contest the routing. As liability: Derek's residual entanglements (financial, criminal, possibly mystical via the Masque-adjacent network) are inherited problems Eleanor cannot fully discharge. Eleanor's relationship to Derek's memory is therefore *operational rather than grieving* — she invokes him when useful, suppresses him when not, and almost certainly experiences his death as net favorable to her position while performing the appropriate widowhood when the audience requires it.

### The dead Eleanor

The version of Eleanor who "died" — the woman whose obituary ran, whose funeral occurred, whose daughters mourned — is a separate position in the file and arguably the most psychologically consequential one. *Eleanor lived for years as her own ghost.* She read about her own death; she may have attended her own memorials in disguise or via proxy; she watched her daughters grieve a woman who was sitting in another city, alive. The sustained enactment of one's own erasure is not a survivable psychological operation without significant cost, and the cost in Eleanor's case is the muscle of full affective registration. The dead Eleanor is the one who could feel what her absence cost Kate. The living, returned Eleanor cannot quite reach that capacity anymore; the channel has scarred over. This is the deepest casualty of the faked-death decision and the one Eleanor herself cannot name, because naming it would require her to grieve a self she chose to kill.

### Trust / Safety / Failure / Leverage

The dead are leverage on Eleanor in the specific sense that *exhumation* — narrative, financial, forensic — would undo her current position. Any operative who reconstructs the faked-death mechanism in detail (the coroner suborned, the body substituted or never produced, the obituary planted, the assets moved, the assumed identity assumed) holds the master key to Eleanor's accountability. Eleanor knows this. The compartmentalization of that period is the tightest compartment in her operational life and the one she defends with the most resource. It is also, by the structural logic of all of Eleanor's defenses, the compartment most likely to be penetrated by *Kate*, because Kate is the only person with both motive and standing to ask the questions that produce the documentation.

---

**Synthesis for this section.** Eleanor's family bonds are arranged in a hub-and-spoke topology with Eleanor at the hub: Derek as the founding spoke (closed by death), Kate as the active and unresolved spoke (under continuous management), Susan as the lateral spoke (cultivated as channel), the elder daughter as the low-temperature spoke (managed by neglect), the family of origin as the missing spoke (suppressed by silence), and the dead-Eleanor as the internal spoke (suppressed by compartmentalization). The architecture is stable so long as no spoke communicates with another outside Eleanor's mediation. The fragility of the architecture is that *Kate is now adult, investigative, and motivated*, and that several of the other spokes hold information Kate has not yet asked for. The family system, in surveillance terms, is one good investigator away from reorganizing without Eleanor at the hub.


## Relationships II — Allies, Chosen Family & Mentors

# RELATIONSHIPS II — ALLIES, CHOSEN FAMILY & MENTORS

A note before the entries. Eleanor Bishop does not have allies in the way her daughter has allies. Kate Bishop accumulates chosen family in the costumed sense — people who would bleed for her without being asked, and who she would bleed for. Eleanor accumulates *positions*: people whose interests have been arranged to overlap with hers for a contractually or socially defined duration. The distinction matters for this section, because what would be a "best friend" entry in an ordinary subject's file is, in Eleanor's, closer to a long-tenured account relationship. The warmth is not false. The warmth is just not the load-bearing element.

What follows maps the figures who function — operationally — in the slots that "ally, chosen family, mentor, protege" occupy in a normal life. Several entries are necessarily reconstructive: Eleanor curates her social field so that very little of it appears in the published record under her own name. Her presence in other people's lives is documented mostly by inference — the lawyer who calls Kate "on behalf of," the charity that mysteriously absorbs a problem, the old friend who appears at a gala with knowledge she could only have gotten from Eleanor.

---

## Susan Bishop — Sister-in-Law, Lateral Confidante, Permanent Counterweight

The most operationally important "ally" in Eleanor's adult life, and the only person in the canonical record who occupies something close to a peer relationship with her over decades.

**History.** Susan entered the family through Derek and stayed in it through his deterioration. By the time Eleanor faked her death, Susan was the only Bishop-adjacent adult with sustained access to Kate, and she remained so through the entire absence period. The published material consistently positions Susan as Kate's de-facto family adult — the one who showed up to events, who could be called — while Derek was emotionally and ethically unavailable. Whatever surveillance and guardrail Eleanor maintained around Kate during the ghost years ran, in part, through Susan, whether Susan understood the arrangement or not.

**Dynamics.** The Susan–Eleanor relationship has the texture of two women who learned each other inside a family run by a man neither of them respected. There is a baseline of female-coded solidarity — the shared eye-roll, the practical division of domestic labor at family functions, the wordless understanding of which Bishop man needs to be steered away from which conversation. Underneath that surface, Eleanor reads Susan with the same instrumentality she reads everyone: Susan is useful, Susan is trustworthy in the specific sense of being predictable, and Susan's loyalty to Kate makes her the perfect unwitting conduit. Eleanor's posture toward Susan is *warm-managerial*. She remembers Susan's preferences, sends the right notes, defers in the right places, and quietly never grants Susan access to the actually load-bearing information.

**Evolution.** During the ghost years, Susan functioned as Kate's substitute. Post-return, Eleanor had to renegotiate her position relative to Susan — Susan had effectively occupied the maternal slot, and a less strategic woman would have either attacked Susan or made a show of gratitude. Eleanor did neither; she absorbed Susan back into a sister-in-law register and acted as if no displacement had occurred, which was a small masterpiece of social re-zoning. After the Masque revelations and Kate's hardening of distance, Susan became the most important *channel* in Eleanor's life — Kate would not take Eleanor's calls but would take Susan's, and Susan would, in the course of normal aunting, transmit information in both directions.

**Trust and safety.** Eleanor's trust of Susan is moderate-to-high in the *behavioral prediction* sense and low in the *secret-keeping* sense. She does not tell Susan anything Susan cannot be allowed to tell Kate. Susan's trust of Eleanor is — based on observable warmth and the absence of canonical rupture — high in the relational register and almost certainly uninformed in the strategic register. Susan does not appear to know the full shape of the Masque arrangement; if she did, the texture of her interactions with Kate would shift, and it has not.

**Failure mode.** The relationship fails if Susan ever assembles the full picture of how Eleanor has used her as a conduit to Kate. Susan is not a woman who would tolerate having been instrumentalized in the parenting of a niece she actually loves. The break, if it came, would be quiet and total — Susan does not stage rupture, she simply stops returning calls — and would cost Eleanor her last line into Kate's daily life.

**Leverage.** Eleanor holds family-financial and social-positional leverage over Susan that she has visibly declined to use, which is itself a form of relationship maintenance. Susan holds, without knowing it, the most important leverage anyone holds over Eleanor: the ability to confirm to Kate, with credibility Eleanor lacks, any account of Eleanor's behavior. One sentence from Susan, believed by Kate, finishes the long argument.

---

## Whitney Frost / Madame Masque — Patron, Counterparty, Mirror

Not an ally in any moral sense, but functionally the most important non-familial relationship in Eleanor's adult ledger and the only peer who knows the actual shape of her.

**History.** The bargain that underwrites Eleanor's faked death and her capacity to maneuver in Kate's adulthood was struck with Madame Masque. The exact terms remain partially redacted in the canonical record, but the structure is unambiguous: Eleanor needed escape and protection; Masque had infrastructure for both; the consideration was — eventually, escalatingly — Kate as available collateral. The relationship is years old by the time Kate becomes aware of it, and the published reveal shows it has been continuously active, not a one-time transaction.

**Dynamics.** Two women who recognize each other. Masque is, in the costumed register, what Eleanor is in the civil register — a woman who survived the men in her formative orbit by converting herself into a managing intelligence and farming everyone else's vulnerability for position. Their interactions, where shown, have the affect of professional respect more than fear; Eleanor does not behave around Masque the way an ordinary civilian behaves around organized crime, and Masque does not condescend to Eleanor the way she condescends to ordinary marks. They negotiate. Each is the only person in the other's life who cannot be out-strategized on the first move.

**Evolution.** What began as a survival bargain matured into a working relationship in which Eleanor became, in effect, a *client of standing* — someone whose ongoing existence and freedom of movement is part of Masque's portfolio. Over time the asymmetry has shifted: in the original deal Eleanor was the supplicant; by the period of the Thompson arcs she is closer to a counterparty whose continued cooperation Masque values. Eleanor has, in other words, paid down enough of the original debt with delivered favors that she has accumulated something like creditworthiness.

**Trust and safety.** Both women trust each other in exactly one sense: each is confident the other will act in her own interest, predictably and competently. Neither trusts the other in any sentimental sense. Eleanor is never safe with Masque; Masque is never safe with Eleanor; and both are content to operate inside that mutual non-safety because the alternative — a relationship requiring sentimental trust — would be more dangerous to each of them than the present arrangement.

**Failure mode.** The relationship fails catastrophically and in one direction: if Eleanor ever ceases to be net-useful to Masque, the protection collapses and the leverage is called in. The relationship also fails — more slowly, more interestingly — if Eleanor's standing with Kate becomes so weak that she can no longer credibly deliver Kate-adjacent value, at which point Eleanor's market value to Masque drops and the terms renegotiate downward.

**Leverage.** Masque holds the original instrument — the documentary and operational evidence of the faked-death arrangement and of every favor since — and could destroy Eleanor's civil life with a single coordinated disclosure. Eleanor holds, in return, only what every long-tenured counterparty holds: knowledge of Masque's own arrangements that Masque would prefer not to see surfaced, and the inconvenience of explaining a dead client. This is a stable, asymmetric, mutually instrumental arrangement of the kind that lasts exactly until it doesn't.

---

## The Retained Counsel — Long-Tenure Attorney(s), Function Over Person

The single most-used "ally" category in Eleanor's day-to-day life, and the one the canonical record gestures at most often without ever naming with full specificity. Treat as a composite operational role.

**History.** Eleanor has had access to top-shelf legal representation continuously, including through the ghost years (the arrangements required to fake her death and manage assets in absentia are not executable without at least one fully informed attorney) and across the reentry period (Kate has been on the receiving end of Eleanor-directed legal communication often enough that a regular channel exists).

**Dynamics.** The relationship is the cleanest in Eleanor's life precisely because it is contractual. Eleanor is, for an attorney of the relevant tier, an ideal client: she pays on time, she briefs clearly, she does not panic, she does not lie to her lawyer, and she does not ask her lawyer to do anything that requires the lawyer to lie under oath. She asks her lawyer to do things that are aggressive, well-papered, and within the wide latitude of what aggressive well-papered counsel will do for a wealthy long-tenured client.

**Evolution.** Likely the same firm or the same small number of professionals across many years; Eleanor does not rotate counsel because rotation creates exposure. Whoever holds the file holds the entire file, which means Eleanor's relationship to that person is one of the small number of relationships in her life that is, structurally, *for keeps*.

**Trust and safety.** High operational trust, bounded by attorney-client privilege and by the fact that the attorney knows enough that any rupture would itself be a legal event. Eleanor is, in this single relationship, willing to disclose materially against her own interest because privilege guarantees containment. This is the closest thing in her life to a confessional.

**Failure mode.** The relationship fails if the attorney dies, retires without a clean transition, or develops a personal moral objection of sufficient force to override professional inertia — most plausibly an objection grounded in something Eleanor asks counsel to do regarding Kate specifically. Counsel will execute many things; counsel will not, indefinitely, execute the weaponization of a child-now-adult against herself.

**Leverage.** Counsel holds everything. Eleanor holds the fee, the long relationship, and the assurance of privilege. The arrangement is stable for the same reason most such arrangements are stable: neither party benefits from disturbing it.

---

## The Charitable / Social Circuit — Board Peers, Gala Acquaintances, "Old Friends"

A category rather than a person, because Eleanor's social field is organized as a *field*, not as a set of individual friendships.

**History.** Eleanor's presence in upper-Manhattan philanthropic and social infrastructure predates the ghost years and resumed after them. The continuity is itself notable — a woman who was understood to be dead for years and then was not should produce more social friction on reentry than Eleanor appears to have produced, which suggests that the relevant social tier accepted her revised account with the studied incuriosity that very wealthy people deploy around each other's unexplained chapters.

**Dynamics.** Eleanor is, in this register, *exemplary*. She is the woman who remembers names, who writes the right notes, who is on the right boards at the right level, who is generous within recognized norms and never embarrassingly more so. She does not dominate rooms. She positions herself at the elbow of whoever does. The function of these relationships is information and standing — knowing who is moving money where, who has fallen out with whom, whose children are in what trouble — and the manufacture of a permanent, plausible, fully documented civilian life.

**Evolution.** Pre-ghost: ordinary high-tier social participation. Ghost-years: necessarily absent under her own name; quite possibly present under another, given her temperament. Post-return: a careful, graded reentry that traded on Derek's continuing position and on the social cover provided by performed widowhood-and-recovery.

**Trust and safety.** Effectively zero in either direction. No one in this circuit is told anything that matters. The relationships are real in that they are reliably maintained; they are not real in the sense that any one of them would survive a real test.

**Failure mode.** The circuit fails as a unit, not individually. If Eleanor's actual operational history — the Masque bargain, the use of Kate — became publicly legible at the dinner-party level, the entire field would cool simultaneously, in the particular way wealth cools: no confrontation, simply a slow attenuation of invitations.

**Leverage.** Diffuse and mutual. Eleanor holds, on each of these acquaintances, the small accumulated knowledge of years of close observation; they hold, on her, the same. The system is stable because everyone is equally exposed.

---

## Domestic and Personal Staff — The Closest Daily Witnesses

The people physically nearest Eleanor on an average day, and the people whose existence in her file is most under-documented in canon and most certain in inference.

**History.** A woman of Eleanor's tier, era, and temperament runs a household with continuous staff: housekeeper(s), driver(s), personal assistant, possibly a long-tenured secretary handling correspondence and calendar. Several of these roles are necessarily long-tenure — Eleanor does not tolerate the security exposure of high turnover among people who see her keys, her phone, her mail, and her unguarded face.

**Dynamics.** Eleanor is, by all available signal, a *good* employer in the technical sense: courteous, on-time with pay, undramatic, clear in expectation. She is not warm in the way that produces lifelong familial attachment between staff and employer; she is correct in the way that produces durable professional loyalty. The boundary is maintained from her side, not theirs.

**Evolution.** The ghost years would have required either dismissing the previous staff cleanly (with sufficient settlement to ensure silence) or read-in continuity with a small core. The latter is more likely for at least one role — a personal assistant or equivalent — because the logistics of the disappearance and the asset management during it require a trusted hand. Post-return staff is likely a mix of pre-ghost continuity (where possible) and carefully vetted new hires.

**Trust and safety.** High in the *they will not steal, will not sell, will not gossip* sense, secured by money, reference networks, and the structural unattractiveness of crossing her. Low in the *she would confide* sense — Eleanor does not confide in anyone she pays.

**Failure mode.** Staff loyalty fails under two conditions: a competing payer offering materially more (rare at this tier, defended against by Eleanor's compensation practices) or a personal moral break caused by witnessing something specific regarding Kate. The latter is the more realistic failure vector. A long-tenured housekeeper who watched Kate grow up has her own opinions about what is owed to Kate, and those opinions could, under sufficient provocation, override professional discretion.

**Leverage.** Eleanor holds wages, references, and the social-class power asymmetry. Staff hold the daily observational record — every call overheard, every visitor logged, every late-night document left on the desk. In any honest accounting, the household staff are the single largest unmonitored intelligence exposure in Eleanor's life.

---

## Kate Bishop — The Relationship That Is Filed Here Because Eleanor Files It Here

Kate appears in the family-relationships register of any sane file. She appears *also* here because Eleanor, in her own internal accounting, classifies Kate as her *most important ally* — the person whose alignment with her would resolve every other open question in her life — and the analytical file must record that misclassification, because it drives behavior.

**Eleanor's operative model of Kate.** Daughter, yes; also: the only person whose endorsement would retroactively legitimize the entire post-ghost project. Eleanor needs Kate not merely as a daughter but as a *witness who agrees*. The pursuit of Kate's reconciliation is, in Eleanor's psychology, the pursuit of her own coherence. This is why she cannot let the estrangement settle: a settled estrangement is a permanent verdict against the self-narrative.

**Dynamics as ally-frame.** Eleanor approaches Kate, repeatedly, with the structure of *partnership offers*: resources, infrastructure, doors opened, problems made to disappear. This is the vocabulary in which Eleanor extends love. Kate, who has built her adult identity precisely on refusing this vocabulary, reads each offer correctly as an attempted enrollment.

**Trust and safety.** Eleanor trusts Kate absolutely in the sense of believing Kate will not cross certain lines (will not destroy her publicly, will not have her killed, will not abandon the title of daughter entirely). Kate trusts Eleanor in the sense of believing Eleanor will, reliably, behave as Eleanor. Neither trusts the other in any sense that would make a real shared project possible.

**Failure mode.** The ally-frame has already failed, in the sense that Kate has refused it. Eleanor continues to operate inside a model where the failure is provisional and the offer remains open. This is the single most stable error in Eleanor's strategic life and the one she is least equipped to correct.

**Leverage.** Mapped exhaustively in other sections; for the purposes of this register, note only that Eleanor's leverage on Kate has continuously *decreased* across the published record while Kate's leverage on Eleanor has continuously *increased*, and that Eleanor has not adjusted her behavior to that shift.

---

## Mentors — A Structural Absence

No mentor figure appears in the canonical record for Eleanor Bishop, and the absence is itself a finding worth filing.

Women of Eleanor's cohort and tier typically display at least one durable mentor relationship in their biographies — an older woman of the same social register who functioned, in young adulthood, as guide and door-opener. The absence of any such figure in Eleanor's record is consistent with the inferred etiology in the psychiatric workup: a family-of-origin in which female agency was routed exclusively through alliance with powerful men, leaving no template for *upward female mentorship* and no instinct to seek it. Eleanor learned the world from the men she married and the men she negotiated with; she did not learn it from a woman she admired.

The operational consequence is that Eleanor has never been the *junior partner* in a relationship with another competent woman who wished her well without wishing to use her. She has no experiential model for what such a relationship feels like. This is why Madame Masque, who wishes her ill while using her, reads to Eleanor as a more *legible* peer than any of her charitable-circuit acquaintances who wish her neither ill nor well. Eleanor recognizes instrumentality. She does not, in any deep way, recognize disinterested goodwill from another woman, and would likely mistake it, if offered, for a slower form of approach.

---

## Proteges — Also Structurally Absent, Also Diagnostic

Eleanor has no protege in the published record. She does not mentor younger women in any of the social or philanthropic structures she occupies; she does not develop staff into successors; she does not — most strikingly — mentor her own daughter, despite having decades of relevant operational expertise that would, in a healthier configuration, be exactly what a mother of her capacities would transmit.

The absence is overdetermined. To mentor is to invest in another person's eventual independent competence; Eleanor's entire interpersonal architecture is organized against the production of independent competence in people she is invested in. The terminal state of mentorship — the moment the protege no longer needs you — is the state Eleanor has spent her life avoiding with every relationship she actually values. She therefore cannot occupy the mentor position without dismantling the mechanism by which she relates to the people she would mentor.

The single partial exception is Kate, whom Eleanor has, at various points across the Thompson arcs, tried to *guide* in matters of casework, finance, and personal entanglement. These attempts uniformly fail because they are not mentorship; they are reabsorption with mentorship as the cover story, and Kate is by now too well-calibrated to Eleanor's frequencies to mistake the one for the other.

---

**Summary of the register.** Eleanor Bishop's "allies, chosen family, and mentors" file is, on inspection, a portfolio of *positions* — a sister-in-law operating as a transmission line, a criminal counterparty operating as a patron, a counsel operating as a confessional, a social circuit operating as cover, a household staff operating as the unwatched watchers, a daughter mis-filed as an ally, and the diagnostic absences where mentors and proteges should be. There is no figure in this register who could be called, without qualification, a *friend*. The closest the record comes is Susan, and Susan would qualify only because Eleanor has not yet had occasion to spend her down. The pathos, again, is that Eleanor experiences this portfolio as a rich life and would, if asked, list several of these people as people she loves — and would not be entirely wrong, because the love is real, and is also never the load-bearing element.


## Relationships III — Rivals, Enemies & Lovers

# RELATIONSHIPS III — RIVALS, ENEMIES & LOVERS

*Eleanor Bishop is a woman whose adversaries and intimates occupy overlapping categories: the people she has slept with, the people she has owed, and the people she has wished dead are not three lists but one ledger viewed from different angles. What follows maps each significant counterparty in the file — every figure with whom Eleanor has shared a bed, a contract, a vendetta, or a mutually destructive secret. Where the canon is explicit, it is cited; where surveillance-grade inference is required, it is marked as such and grounded in observed pattern.*

---

## A. INTIMATES & LOVERS

### A.1 — Derek Bishop (husband; primary marital counterparty; deceased per current canon)

The defining relationship of Eleanor's adult life, and the template against which every later entanglement should be read. Derek and Eleanor's marriage was, in the surveillance record, a *binational alliance of two adjacent corruptions* — old-money Bishop publishing, old-money Bishop predation, a household where the wine cellar and the off-book ledger were inventoried by the same accountant.

**History.** The marriage predates Kate's birth by an unknown but substantial interval — long enough for both partners to have established their position in the arrangement before any child arrived to complicate it. The early phase was likely functional in the way such marriages are functional: shared social calendar, shared accountants, shared discretion regarding each other's affairs. Eleanor's affect when she speaks of the early marriage (in the Thompson-era confessions to Kate) is rehearsed-warm in a manner that suggests the warmth is itself an artifact of later reframing — she needs the marriage to have had a happy phase in order for her escape narrative to function morally.

**Dynamics.** Power inside the marriage was asymmetrical in the *direction Eleanor would later weaponize* — Derek's name, Derek's signature on the larger instruments, Derek's relationship with the underworld counterparties. But Eleanor was not a kept wife; she was a *co-signatory who chose to be quiet about it*. The surveillance signature of their interactions, reconstructible from staff testimony and Kate's childhood memory, is: separate breakfasts, joint dinners only when guests required it, household-staff communication routed through Eleanor not Derek, all checks above a threshold counter-signed by both.

**Evolution.** What broke the marriage is contested in Eleanor's own telling and was retconned by Thompson into something darker. The publicly offered version — Derek became dangerous, Eleanor fled into a faked death to protect Kate — is contradicted by the Masque-arc revelations, which establish Eleanor as a co-author of the corruption rather than its refugee. The truer reading: the marriage decayed when Derek became *unmanageable* by Eleanor's standards. He took risks she would not have sanctioned, made enemies who would reach into the household, stopped being a counterparty she could negotiate with and started being a liability she could not contain. Her response was not divorce — divorce would not have severed the financial and criminal entanglements — but *erasure*, a much more expensive solution that left Derek alive and exposed while Eleanor went underground with the assets she had quietly migrated.

**Trust/safety.** Effectively zero in the operative period. Eleanor's documents, her separate accounts, her later Masque alliance all suggest she had been treating Derek as a hostile counterparty for years before she vanished. The household had the surface of marriage and the internal architecture of a tense joint venture between two principals who each kept files on the other.

**Failure mode.** The marriage failed at the precise point Eleanor could no longer convert Derek's behavior into manageable risk. She does not appear to have grieved him in any sense recognizable to non-Eleanor observers; she appears to have *closed the position*.

**Leverage (mutual, historic).** Derek held her name, her social position, and presumably documentation of her own involvements. Eleanor held the household's actual operational knowledge — who was paid, who was owed, where the bodies (figurative; possibly literal) were filed. The Masque deal can be read as Eleanor liquidating Derek's leverage by inserting a counterparty bigger than him into the equation.

---

### A.2 — Jack Duquesne (fiancé; current/ongoing entanglement in the late-period record)

*Note: the Duquesne engagement is a thread Eleanor maintains in the later Thompson and adjacent material; the relationship is shaped by the same pattern as the Derek marriage but with the variables rotated.*

**History.** Eleanor's relationship with Duquesne is presented to Kate as a romance and to the outside world as a remarriage in progress. It surfaces after Eleanor's "return" from the faked death and is, by the structural logic of Eleanor's life, almost certainly *not primarily romantic in motive*. Jack arrives in Eleanor's orbit at a moment when she requires (a) social cover for her reentry, (b) a new male counterparty through whom certain Duquesne-family-adjacent assets and contacts can be accessed, and (c) a fresh narrative for Kate — "your mother has moved on, your mother is happy, your mother is normal."

**Dynamics.** Eleanor performs affection toward Jack with high fidelity in public settings — hand placement, eye-line, the small touches at the small of the back during photographable moments. The private signature, where it has been observable, is markedly cooler: separate sleeping arrangements inferable from staff schedules, conversations conducted across furniture rather than within arm's reach, the specific way Eleanor will end a Jack-initiated topic by complimenting his tie. The compliment is the period at the end of his sentence.

**Evolution.** The relationship's trajectory is one of *increasing instrumentalization disguised as deepening commitment*. As Kate's resistance to Eleanor hardens, Eleanor leans harder on the Jack-frame — the engagement becomes a recurring reason to draw Kate into family events, to require her presence at planning meetings, to position her as a daughter-of-the-bride and thus inside a familial structure she would otherwise refuse.

**Trust/safety.** Eleanor does not trust Jack with anything that matters; she trusts him to perform his role. Jack's own awareness of his role's instrumental nature is the open question — the surveillance read is that he is partially aware, partially in love with the version of Eleanor she shows him, and entirely outmatched in the negotiation he does not know he is in.

**Failure mode.** This pairing will end the way Eleanor's primary partnerships end: at the moment Jack becomes more expensive to manage than to discard, either because his own legal-criminal entanglements surface to threaten Eleanor's reentry, or because Kate weaponizes him against her. Eleanor will not grieve. She will close the position.

**Leverage.** Eleanor holds the social momentum and the optics; Jack holds family-adjacent access Eleanor needs but does not require permanently. The leverage ratio favors Eleanor and grows more favorable monthly.

---

### A.3 — Inferential sexual/romantic history outside marriage

The canonical record is silent on Eleanor's affairs during the marriage and during the faked-death years, which is itself evidentially significant. A woman of Eleanor's class, age, attractiveness, and operational mobility, married to Derek Bishop, *and* sustaining a multi-year underground identity, almost certainly maintained intimate relationships outside the marriage. The absence of any such figure in the surfaced record indicates one of two patterns:

1. **Disciplined abstinence as operational hygiene** — Eleanor declines to introduce additional counterparties into her life because each lover is a potential leak. This is consistent with her cognitive style (transactional, long-horizon, ledger-aware).
2. **Lovers were entirely fungible and unrecorded** — engagements with men (or women) whose function was discharged in single or limited encounters, never permitted to ascend to the status of *position* in the portfolio.

Either reading is consistent with her profile. What is *not* consistent with her profile is a hidden grand passion; Eleanor does not have the affective architecture for one. The romantic capacity has been re-tasked to other uses.

---

## B. RIVALS & ENEMIES

### B.1 — Whitney Frost / Madame Masque (the defining adversary-creditor)

The single most important non-familial relationship in Eleanor's life, and the one whose terms continue to structure her behavior even after the immediate arc resolves.

**History.** Eleanor's bargain with Madame Masque is the hinge of the Thompson-era revelations. The bargain's terms, reconstructed from the published arc: Masque provided some combination of (a) the means or cover for Eleanor's faked death, (b) protection from Derek and his counterparties during the underground years, (c) liquidity at a moment Eleanor's own resources were frozen or compromised. In exchange, Eleanor offered — *and this is the moral core of the file* — a position on Kate. Not Kate's body in the immediate sense, but Kate as collateral: a future claim, an access right, a name on a ledger Masque could call when she chose.

**Dynamics.** Eleanor and Masque are, by personality, the *same animal at different volumes*. Both are competent women operating in worlds designed to exclude or instrumentalize them; both have built power through the management of dangerous men; both understand the conversion of intimacy into leverage as a basic life skill. The difference is presentational: Masque wears the mask, Eleanor wears the warmth. Their interactions, where depicted, have a quality of *professional courtesy among peers who would each prefer the other dead but recognize the inefficiency of arranging it.*

**Evolution.** The relationship evolves from *patron/client* (Masque as creditor, Eleanor as supplicant) to *entangled co-belligerents* (each holding enough on the other that direct conflict is mutually destructive) to *managed standoff* (the current state — Masque has been partially neutralized as an immediate threat, but the ledger has not been zeroed). Eleanor's later behaviors — the rapidity with which she tries to consolidate control over Kate, the urgency under the warmth — are partly a function of needing Kate close enough to defend before Masque or her successors decide to call the position.

**Trust/safety.** Negative. Eleanor and Masque each know enough about the other to require the other's continued existence to be conditional on continued silence. Eleanor's protective behaviors toward Kate, where genuine, are partly maternal and partly *defense of an asset already pledged to a hostile counterparty*.

**Failure mode (Eleanor's perspective).** The Masque relationship fails — for Eleanor — at the moment the bargain becomes public to Kate in its full structure. As long as Kate believes Eleanor was a victim of Masque, Eleanor retains the moral position from which to attempt reconciliation. The day Kate produces the actual terms of the bargain in plain language, that position closes.

**Leverage.** Symmetrical and high. Masque can produce documentation or testimony that ends Eleanor's social and possibly legal viability. Eleanor presumably possesses reciprocal material on Masque's operations during the period of their alliance. Mutually assured exposure. Neither has incentive to fire first; both have incentive to keep the other in play as an insurance instrument.

---

### B.2 — Kate Bishop *as adversary* (the most painful entry in this section)

Kate appears in this section as well as in Relationships I/II because the late-period record establishes her, structurally, as Eleanor's most consequential rival — the only counterparty Eleanor cannot manage, cannot buy, cannot outlast, and cannot replace.

**History.** Kate spent her first life-phase as Eleanor's absence and her second as Eleanor's project. The third — current — phase is Kate as Eleanor's *opposition research subject*. Eleanor monitors Kate's address changes, her financial movements (to the extent legacy access permits), her romantic involvements (Eleanor knows about America Chavez; Eleanor knows about the on-and-off thing with Clint Barton-adjacent figures; Eleanor knows the West Coast roster by name), her case selection, her press footprint.

**Dynamics.** This is the only relationship in Eleanor's life in which she is consistently *outclassed at her own game*. Kate has Eleanor's strategic intelligence, Eleanor's pattern recognition, Eleanor's capacity for long-horizon planning — and is using all of it against Eleanor while declining to be drawn into the maternal frame. Eleanor's stress under this is what produces the tightening behaviors profiled in Section 3: the increased gift-giving, the lawyer-letters, the indirect approaches through Susan.

**Evolution.** The trajectory is one of *Kate's increasing refusal to be a counterparty Eleanor recognizes*. Early Kate negotiated; current Kate does not return calls. Eleanor's adaptive response — escalating the warmth, then escalating the structure, then escalating the intermediaries — has not worked, which is novel data for Eleanor and which she has not yet metabolized.

**Trust/safety (Eleanor toward Kate).** Eleanor genuinely does not believe Kate would harm her. She does believe Kate would *expose* her, which is the same outcome by a different mechanism, and which Eleanor cannot bring herself to plan against because planning against it requires admitting Kate is an adversary, which collapses the maternal self-narrative.

**Failure mode.** The relationship's terminal state, if Eleanor's pattern holds, is one in which Eleanor attempts a last-ditch *grand gesture* — confession, restitution, the public sacrifice of a smaller secret to protect a larger one — and Kate, recognizing the gesture as itself an arrangement, refuses to grant it the weight Eleanor needs. Eleanor will experience this as the most catastrophic loss of her life and will continue to experience it that way without changing the behavior that produced it.

**Leverage (Eleanor's hand against Kate).** Diminishing. Money matters less to Kate every year. Social position matters less than ever. The remaining hooks are sentimental — childhood objects, photographs, Susan-as-conduit, the holiday calendar — and Kate is systematically defusing each one. Eleanor's strongest residual leverage is the threat (never spoken) that her own destruction would catch Kate in the blast radius, and Kate knows this, and is calculating tolerances.

---

### B.3 — Derek Bishop's surviving criminal counterparties (collective)

A class of enemies rather than a single figure: the men and entities to whom Derek owed money, favors, or silence at the time of his death and Eleanor's prior disappearance. Some have been resolved (settled, dead, neutralized through Masque-era arrangements). A non-trivial number remain.

**Dynamics.** Eleanor's strategy with this class is *patient quiet*. She does not engage; she pays through intermediaries when forced; she has, in surveillance-inferred behavior, allowed certain small claims to be settled at face value rather than disputed, to keep her name out of any document that would require a signature.

**Trust/safety.** None. These counterparties are not enemies in the affective sense — they bear her no personal animosity — but they are *standing structural threats* whose dormancy is conditional on her continued ability to manage them at distance.

**Failure mode.** A single one of these figures, sufficiently leveraged by Kate's vigilante work or by a third party's investigation, decides that producing what they know about Eleanor is worth more than continued silence. Eleanor's defenses against this scenario are surveillance, early-warning legal monitoring, and a quietly maintained capacity to leave the country with prepared documentation on short notice.

**Leverage.** Eleanor's leverage against this class is largely financial and reputational — she can pay, she can introduce reputational consequences, she can in extremis route them toward Masque-adjacent enforcers. The leverage is sufficient for management but not for resolution.

---

### B.4 — Susan Bishop (sister-in-law; ambiguous-affiliation rival)

Susan occupies the unusual position of *family member who functions as both proxy and competitor*. The relationship between the two women is mapped more fully in earlier relationship sections, but its adversarial dimension belongs here.

**Dynamics.** Eleanor uses Susan as a channel to Kate when direct channels are closed; Susan uses Eleanor's need for that channel as her own form of standing in the family. Each woman is aware of the other's instrumental use of her. Their interactions have the brittle warmth of two professionals who must collaborate on a long-running project neither will admit they dislike.

**Trust/safety.** Calibrated. Eleanor trusts Susan to behave predictably and to value her own position enough not to torch the household; she does not trust Susan with anything Susan could use to advance against her.

**Failure mode.** Susan, presented with sufficient incentive — financial, reputational, or sentimental on Kate's behalf — defects. Eleanor's defense is to keep the incentive structure such that defection costs Susan more than loyalty. This is sustainable but not permanent.

**Leverage.** Bilateral and small. Each holds enough on the other to require continued courtesy; neither holds enough to require capitulation. The relationship is a stable low-trust equilibrium.

---

### B.5 — Clint Barton (rival-by-proxy; Kate's mentor)

Eleanor does not have a personal conflict with Barton; she has a *structural* one. He is the adult male figure who occupies the developmental space Eleanor's absence vacated and her return cannot reclaim. Every story Kate tells in which Clint matters is a story in which Eleanor does not.

**Dynamics.** Eleanor's surface posture toward Clint is *gracious condescension* — she has met him, she has been polite, she has registered his class register and filed it. Her operative posture is closer to *jealousy expressed as concern* — she raises questions about his fitness, his judgment, his exposure of Kate to danger, with the particular intonation of a parent whose own absence created the vacancy Clint filled.

**Trust/safety.** Eleanor does not consider Clint a threat to her physically. She considers him a threat to her narrative — every demonstration of competent, non-instrumental care from Clint toward Kate is evidence against Eleanor's self-image.

**Failure mode.** Clint, asked directly by Kate to assess Eleanor, gives an honest answer. Eleanor cannot defend against this and does not try; she simply absorbs the loss and increases pressure elsewhere.

**Leverage.** Eleanor has no leverage on Clint that she would be willing to use, because using it would require Kate to learn she had used it. This is, for Eleanor, an unusual condition — a counterparty she cannot move against. She manages it by behaving, in Clint's presence, with the precise civility of a woman storing data for a use case she has not yet identified.

---

### B.6 — America Chavez (rival-by-proxy; Kate's chosen family)

Functionally adjacent to the Barton entry but more acute, because America's bond with Kate is peer-level and elective rather than mentor-shaped. Eleanor reads America correctly as *uncoachable* — a counterparty who cannot be bought, charmed, or routed through intermediaries, who responds to manipulation by naming it immediately and loudly, and whose loyalty to Kate operates on a register Eleanor does not have access to.

**Dynamics.** Eleanor's recorded behavior in America's presence is minimal and stiff. She does not attempt the warmth she deploys with Jack or with Kate's other associates; she has correctly identified that the warmth would be detected and rejected, and she does not waste deployments.

**Trust/safety.** Low and mutual. America has no use for Eleanor; Eleanor has no purchase on America.

**Failure mode.** America, present in a room when Eleanor attempts a manipulation of Kate, *narrates the manipulation in real time*. Eleanor has no defense against this scenario and avoids it by not attempting moves in rooms America occupies.

**Leverage.** Effectively none in either direction. This is the cleanest adversarial relationship in Eleanor's file — clean because nothing is exchanged, nothing is owed, and nothing is pretended.

---

### B.7 — Law enforcement, regulators, and journalists (the diffuse institutional adversary)

Eleanor's relationship to formal institutions is the relationship of a person who has spent decades ensuring she is *adjacent to* rather than *named in* any document that matters. She has retained counsel continuously; she has used multiple firms to fragment what any one firm knows; she has been a quiet philanthropic donor to causes whose patrons include the kind of people one wants on speed dial.

**Dynamics.** Eleanor does not fear law enforcement in the immediate sense — her direct criminal exposure is well-laundered. She fears *the journalist with the long memory*, *the regulator with a grudge*, *the cold-case detective with budget*. Her behavior against this class is preemptive: charitable boards, lunch calendars, the named-donor wing.

**Trust/safety.** She trusts the institutions to behave institutionally — predictably, slowly, expensively. She does not trust individuals within them.

**Failure mode.** A single sufficiently motivated investigator, working in coordination with Kate or with one of Derek's old counterparties, produces a coherent narrative the institutions can act on. Eleanor's defense is the prepared exit and the maintained cover.

**Leverage.** Modest and structural. She is not powerful enough to quash a serious investigation, but she is positioned enough to slow one and to be warned about one. This is sufficient for the life she currently leads. It would not be sufficient if the conditions changed.

---

## C. SUMMATIVE NOTE

Across every entry in this section, the pattern that emerges is the one identified in the psychological workup: *Eleanor does not have enemies in the way most people have enemies, and she does not have lovers in the way most people have lovers.* She has **counterparties**. Each person in this file occupies a position in a portfolio she maintains with continuous low-grade attention, and the affective coloration of each position — warmth, fear, civility, jealousy, courtesy — is a function of what the position requires for its management, not of what Eleanor independently feels.

The diagnostic exception is Kate, who is simultaneously the largest position in the portfolio and the one Eleanor cannot manage. This is why Kate appears in every section of the file and will continue to: she is the variable that breaks the model, and Eleanor's continued attempt to force her back into it is the engine of every behavior catalogued above.


## Powers, Abilities & Their Limits

# POWERS, ABILITIES & THEIR LIMITS — ELEANOR BISHOP

## Preamble: The Civilian in a Costumed Field

Eleanor Bishop has no powers in the registered sense — no gamma exposure, no mystic patronage, no serum, no symbiote, no training in the combat arts that define the Marvel ledger. She has never thrown a punch on the page. She has never been struck. She does not run, climb, shoot, drive evasively, or pick a lock. By the somatic taxonomy this dossier usually applies to enhanced subjects, Eleanor scores at or near civilian baseline across every physical axis.

This is the analytically interesting fact. Eleanor has survived — and for long stretches *prospered* — inside the same threat environment that has crippled, killed, or institutionalized superhumans an order of magnitude more capable than herself. She has outlived a husband connected to organized crime; she has been a contracted counterparty of Whitney Frost, a figure whose other counterparties tend to end the year shorter or dead; she has been the target of investigative pressure from a costumed daughter trained by Clinton Barton and operating alongside the Young Avengers and the West Coast Avengers. None of these threat-actors has neutralized her. Her capability profile must therefore be read as a *non-physical operating system* — a stack of competences that substitute for power and, in her hands, occasionally exceed it.

What follows is a granular inventory of that stack: mechanism, range, ceiling, growth curve, signature deployments, and the diagnostic reading of each capability as a *symptom-as-signature* — the way each ability also exposes the pathology that produced it.

---

## 1. CAPITAL AS PROSTHETIC

**Mechanism.** Eleanor's primary "power" is the deployable Bishop family fortune and, more specifically, her granular operational control over its discretionary instruments — trusts, holding companies, charitable vehicles, retained professional services, and the social access that money in that quantum unlocks. She does not possess wealth the way Derek possessed it (as an inheritance to spend); she possesses it the way a fund manager possesses it (as positions to move).

**Range.** Effectively global within civilian legal-financial space. She can retain counsel in any jurisdiction Kate operates in; she can fund a competing bidder for a property Kate wants; she can route a charitable donation to a board on which Kate's romantic partner sits. The range is the range of money in a globalized financial system, which is to say nearly total within the law and broad even at its edges.

**Limits.** Capital fails against (a) opponents who are themselves capitalized or extra-legal in ways money cannot reach (Madame Masque is the in-canon proof — Eleanor's money is what bound her to Masque, not what freed her); (b) opponents who refuse the instrument's frame, which is precisely Kate's mature posture; (c) time-critical physical threats — money cannot intercept an arrow in flight. Capital is also self-revealing: every deployment leaves a paper trail Eleanor must then manage.

**Growth.** Across the published arcs Eleanor moves from *spending money* (the early-marriage register) to *structuring money* (the years of her "death") to *weaponizing money relationally* (the return arcs). The growth curve is one of increasing indirection — by the late material she rarely writes a check that is legible as her check.

**Signature application.** The hiring of Kate herself as a private investigator in the Thompson run is the canonical example: Eleanor uses the instrument of a retainer to purchase access, framing, and narrative control simultaneously. Kate is paid to investigate matters Eleanor has pre-shaped; the money is the leash and the smokescreen at once.

**Symptom-as-signature.** Capital deployment is Eleanor's love language because it is the only language in which she can speak without losing control of the speech. A gift of money is an instruction issued in the grammar of affection. The pathology — the inability to tolerate Kate's separateness — finds in money its perfect medium, because money creates ongoing relationships of accounting that intimacy alone does not.

---

## 2. LEGAL AND PROFESSIONAL-SERVICES ORCHESTRATION

**Mechanism.** Eleanor does not personally practice law, accountancy, private intelligence, public relations, or executive protection. She *retains, briefs, and coordinates* practitioners of each. Her skill is the client-side skill of knowing what to ask for, whom to ask, what to withhold from the brief, how to firewall instructions between firms so that no single counsel sees the whole shape, and how to terminate a relationship before it learns enough to be dangerous.

**Range.** As broad as her capital, with the added reach of professional networks that money cannot directly buy but reputation can introduce. Eleanor moves comfortably in the rooms where senior partners, family-office principals, and discreet investigators take meetings.

**Limits.** Bound by what professionals will do for a client — narrower than what Masque's enforcers will do, broader than what most civilians can summon. Bound also by the inevitability of professionals talking to each other; Eleanor's compartmentation is a constant effortful labor, not a structural condition. She must remember which firm knows what.

**Growth.** Pre-"death" Eleanor likely operated through Derek's retained counsel — a passenger in the legal architecture. The faked-death period required her to *originate* the architecture, almost certainly with Masque-adjacent referrals at first and graduating to her own retained stable. By the return arcs she runs the orchestra; this is observable in the speed with which she produces lawyers around Kate the moment Kate's autonomy looks dangerous.
